<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg</small></span> cites two eminent domain cases from the 1890s to support the proposition that our case law does not "toe the activity versus inactivity line."  <em>Post</em>, at 24-25 (citing <em>Monongahela Nav. Co.</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 148 U.&nbsp;S. 312, 335-337 (1893), and <em>Cherokee Nation</em> v. <em>Southern Kansas R. Co.</em>, 135 U.&nbsp;S. 641, 657-659 (1890)).  The fact that the Fifth Amendment requires the payment of just compensation  when the Government exercises its power of eminent domain does not turn the taking into a commercial transaction between the landowner and the Government, let alone a government-compelled transaction between the landowner and a third party.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In an attempt to recast the individual mandate as a regulation of commercial activity, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg</small></span> suggests that "[a]n individual who opts not to purchase insurance from a private insurer can be seen as actively selecting another form of insurance: self-insurance."  <em>Post</em>, at 26.  But "self-insurance" is, in this context, nothing more than a description of the failure to purchase insurance.  Individuals are no more "activ[e] in the self-insurance market" when they fail to purchase insurance, <em>ibid.</em>, than they are active in the "rest" market when doing nothing.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Sotelo, </em>in particular, would seem to refute the joint dissent's contention that we have "never" treated an exaction as a tax if it was denominated a penalty.  <em>Post, </em>at 20.  We are not persuaded by the dissent's attempt to distinguish <em>Sotelo</em> as a statutory construction case from the bankruptcy context.  <em>Post, </em>at 17, n.&nbsp;5.  The dissent itself treats the question here as one of statutory interpretation, and indeed also relies on a statutory interpretation case from the bankruptcy context.  <em>Post, </em>at 23 (citing <em>United States</em> v. <em>Reorganized CF&amp;I Fabricators of Utah</em>, <em>Inc.</em>, 518 U.&nbsp;S. 213, 224 (1996)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In 2016, for example, individuals making $35,000 a year are expected to owe the IRS about $60 for any month in which they do not have health insurance.  Someone with an annual income of $100,000 a year would likely owe about $200.  The price of a qualifying insurance policy is projected to be around $400 per month.  See D. Newman, CRS Report for Congress, Individual Mandate and Related Information Re-quirements Under PPACA 7, and n.&nbsp;25 (2011).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We do not suggest that any exaction lacking a scienter requirement and enforced by the IRS is within the taxing power.  See <em>post, </em>at 23-24 (joint opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kennedy</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, JJ., dissenting).  Congress could not, for example, expand its authority to impose criminal fines by creating strict liability offenses enforced by the IRS rather than the FBI.  But the fact the exaction here is paid like a tax, to the agency that collects taxes--rather than, for example, exacted by Department of Labor inspectors after ferreting out willful malfeasance--suggests that this exaction may be viewed as a tax.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The joint dissent attempts to distinguish <em>New York</em> v. <em>United States</em> on the ground that the seemingly imperative language in that case was in an "introductory provision" that had "no legal consequences."  <em>Post, </em>at 19. <em> </em>We did not rely on that reasoning in <em>New York</em>.  See 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 169-170.  Nor could we have.  While the Court quoted only the broad statement that "[e]ach State shall be responsible" for its waste, that language was implemented through operative provisions that also use the words on which the dissent relies.  See 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 2021e(e)(1) (entitled "Requirements for non-sited compact regions and non-member States" and directing that those entities "shall comply with the following requirements"); 2021e(e)(2) (describing "Penalties for failure to comply").  The Court upheld those provisions not as lawful commands, but as "incentives."  See 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 152-153, 171-173.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Of course, individuals do not have a lawful choice not to pay a tax due, and may sometimes face prosecution for failing to do so (although not for declining to make the shared responsibility payment, see 26 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 5000A(g)(2)).  But that does not show that the tax restricts the lawful choice whether to undertake or forgo the activity on which the tax is predicated.  Those subject to the individual mandate may lawfully forgo health insurance and pay higher taxes, or buy health insurance and pay lower taxes.  The only thing they may not lawfully do is not buy health insurance and not pay the resulting tax.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg</small></span> observes that state Medicaid spending will increase by only 0.8 percent after the expansion.  <em>Post, </em>at 43.<em>  </em>That not only ignores increased state administrative expenses, but also assumes that the Federal Government will continue to fund the expansion at the current statutorily specified levels.  It is not unheard of, however, for the Federal Government to increase requirements in such a manner as to impose unfunded mandates on the States.  More importantly, the size of the new financial burden imposed on a State is irrelevant in analyzing whether the State has been coerced into accepting that burden.  "Your money or your life" is a coercive proposition, whether you have a single dollar in your pocket or $500.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Nor, of course, can the number of pages the amendment occupies, or the extent to which the change preserves and works within the existing program, be dispositive.  Cf. <em>post, </em>at 49-50 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg, J.</small></span>).  Take, for example, the following hypothetical amendment: "All of a State's citizens are now eligible for Medicaid."  That change would take up a single line and would not alter any "operational aspect[&nbsp;] of the program" beyond the eligibility requirements.  <em>Post, </em>at 49.  Yet it could hardly be argued that such an amendment was a permissible modification of Medicaid, rather than an attempt to foist an entirely new health care system upon the States.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg</small></span> suggests that the States can have no objection to the Medicaid expansion, because "Congress could have repealed Medicaid [and,] [t]hereafter, .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. could have enacted Medicaid II, a new program combining the pre-2010 coverage with the expanded coverage required by the ACA."  <em>Post</em>, at 51; see also <em>post,</em> at 38.  But it would certainly not be that easy.  Practical constraints would plainly inhibit, if not preclude, the Federal Government from repealing the existing program and putting every feature of Medicaid on the table for political reconsideration.  Such a massive undertaking would hardly be "ritualistic."  <em>Ibid.</em>  The same is true of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg</small></span>'s suggestion that Congress could establish Medicaid as an exclusively federal program.  <em>Post,</em> at 44.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;According to one study conducted by the National Center for Health Statistics, the high cost of insurance is the most common reason why individuals lack coverage, followed by loss of one's job, an employer's unwillingness to offer insurance or an insurers' unwillingness to cover those with preexisting medical conditions, and loss of Medicaid coverage.  See Dept. of Health and Human Services, National Center for Health Statistics, Summary Health Statistics for the U.&nbsp;S. Population: National Health Interview Survey--2009, Ser. 10, No. 248, p. 71, Table 25 (Dec. 2010).  "[D]id not want or need coverage" received too few responses to warrant its own category.  See <em>ibid., </em>n.&nbsp;2.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Despite its success, Massachusetts' medical-care providers still administer substantial amounts of uncompensated care, much of that to uninsured patients from out-of-state.  See <em>supra,</em> at 7-8.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Alexander Hamilton described the problem this way:  "[Often] it would be beneficial to all the states to encourage, or suppress[,] a particular branch of trade, while it would be detrimental .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. to attempt it without the concurrence of the rest."  The Continentalist No. V, in 3 Papers of Alexander Hamilton<em> </em>75, 78 (H. Syrett ed. 1962).  Because the concurrence of all States was exceedingly difficult to obtain, Hamilton observed, "the experiment would probably be left untried."  <em>Ibid.</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Dept. of Health and Human Services, National Center for Health Statistics, Summary Health Statistics for U.&nbsp;S. Adults: National Health Interview Survey 2009, Ser. 10, No. 249, p. 124, Table 37 (Dec.&nbsp;2010).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Echoing <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice,</small></span> the joint dissenters urge that the minimum coverage provision impermissibly regulates young people who "have no intention of purchasing [medical care]" and are too far "removed from the [health-care] market."  See <em>post, </em>at 8, 11.  This criticism ignores the reality that a healthy young person may be a day away from needing health care.  See <em>supra,</em> at 4.  A victim of an accident or unforeseen illness will consume extensive medical care immediately, though scarcely expecting to do so.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span>'s reliance on the quoted passages of the Constitution, see <em>ante, </em>at 18-19, is also dubious on other grounds.  The power to "regulate the Value" of the national currency presumably includes the power to increase the currency's worth--<em>i.e., </em>to create value where none previously existed.  And if the power to "[r]egulat[e] .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. the land and naval Forces" presupposes "there is already [in existence] something to be regulated," <em>i.e., </em>an Army and a Navy, does Congress lack authority to create an Air Force?</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span>'s characterization of individuals who choose not to purchase private insurance as "doing nothing," <em>ante, </em>at 20, is similarly questionable.  A person who self-insures opts against prepayment for a product the person will in time consume.  When aggregated, exercise of that option has a substantial impact on the health-care market.  See <em>supra,</em> at 5-7, 16-17.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Some adherents to the joint dissent have questioned the existence of substantive due process rights.  See <em>McDonald</em> v. <em>Chicago</em>, 561 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2010) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., concurring) (slip op., at 7) (The notion that the Due Process Clause "could define the substance of th[e] righ[t to liberty] strains credulity."); <em>Albright</em> v. <em>Oliver</em>, 510 U.&nbsp;S. 266, 275 (1994) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia</small></span>, J., concurring) ("I reject the proposition that the Due Process Clause guarantees certain (unspecified) liberties[.]").  Given these Justices' reluctance to interpret the Due Process Clause as guaranteeing liberty interests, their willingness to plant such protections in the Commerce Clause is striking.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The failure to purchase vegetables in <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice'</small></span>s<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> </small></span>hypothetical, then, is <em>not</em> what leads to higher health-care costs for others; rather, it is the failure of individuals to maintain a healthy diet, and the resulting obesity, that creates the cost-shifting problem.  See <em>ante, </em>at<em> </em>22-23.  Requiring individuals to purchase vegetables is thus several steps removed from solving the problem.  The failure to obtain health insurance, by contrast, is the <em>immediate cause </em>of the cost-shifting Congress sought to address through the ACA.  See <em>supra,</em> at 5-7.  Requiring individuals to obtain insurance attacks the source of the problem directly, in a single step.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indeed, Congress regularly and uncontroversially requires individuals who are "doing nothing," see <em>ante, </em>at 20, to take action.  Examples include federal requirements to report for jury duty, 28 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1866(g) (2006 ed., Supp. IV); to register for selective service, 50 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. App. 453; to purchase firearms and gear in anticipation of service in the Militia, 1 Stat. 271 (Uniform Militia Act of 1792); to turn gold currency over to the Federal Government in exchange for paper currency, see <em>Nortz</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 294 U.&nbsp;S. 317, 328 (1935); and to file a tax return, 26 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 6012 (2006 ed., Supp. IV).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In a separate argument, the joint dissenters contend that the minimum coverage provision is not necessary and proper because it was not the "only .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. way" Congress could have made the guaranteed-issue and community-rating reforms work.  <em>Post, </em>at 9-10.  Congress could also have avoided an insurance-market death spiral, the dissenters maintain, by imposing a surcharge on those who did not previously purchase insurance when those individuals eventually enter the health-insurance system.  <em>Post, </em>at 10.  Or Congress could "den[y] a full income tax credit" to those who do not purchase insurance.  <em>Ibid.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Neither a surcharge on those who purchase insurance nor the denial of a tax credit to those who do not would solve the problem created by guaranteed-issue and community-rating requirements.  Neither would prompt the purchase of insurance before sickness or injury occurred.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But even assuming there were "practicable" alternatives to the minimum coverage provision, "we long ago rejected the view that the Necessary and Proper Clause demands that an Act of Congress be '<em>absolutely</em> necessary' to the exercise of an enumerated power."  <em>Jinks</em> v. <em>Richland County</em>, 538 U.&nbsp;S. 456, 462 (2003) (quoting <em>McCulloch</em> v. <em>Maryland</em>, 4 Wheat. 316, 414-415 (1819)).  Rather, the statutory provision at issue need only be "conducive" and "[reasonably] adapted" to the goal Congress seeks to achieve.  <em>Jinks</em>, 538 U.&nbsp;S., at 462 (internal quotation marks omitted).  The minimum coverage provision meets this requirement.  See <em>supra,</em> at 31-33.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice </small></span>states that he must evaluate the constitutionality of the minimum coverage provision under the Commerce Clause because the provision "reads more naturally as a command to buy insurance than as a tax."  <em>Ante, </em>at 44.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> ultimately concludes, however, that interpreting the provision as a tax is a "fairly possible" construction.  <em>Ante,</em> at 32 (internal quotation marks omitted).  That being so, I see no reason to undertake a Commerce Clause analysis that is not outcome determinative.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Medicaid was "plainly an extension of the existing Kerr-Mills" grant program.  Huberfeld, Federalizing Medicaid, 14 U.&nbsp;Pa. J. Const. L. 431, 444-445 (2011).  Indeed, the "section of the Senate report dealing with Title XIX"--the title establishing Medicaid--"was entitled, 'Improvement and Extension of Kerr-Mills Medical Assistance Program.'&nbsp;"  Stevens &amp; Stevens, Welfare Medicine in America 51 (1974) (quoting S.&nbsp;Rep. No. 404, 89th Cong., 1st Sess., pt. 1, p.&nbsp;9 (1965)).  Setting the pattern for Medicaid, Kerr-Mills reimbursed States for a portion of the cost of health care provided to welfare recipients if States met conditions specified in the federal law, <em>e.g., </em>participating States were obliged to offer minimum coverage for hospitalization and physician services.  See Huberfeld, <em>supra,</em> at 443-444.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Available online at http://www.cms.gov/Research-Statistics-Dataand-Systems/Statistics-Trends-and-Reports/NationalHealthExpendData/ NationalHealthAccountsHistorical.html.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Even the study on which the plaintiffs rely, see Brief for Petitioners 10, concludes that "[w]hile most states will experience some increase in spending, this is quite small relative to the federal matching payments and low relative to the costs of uncompensated care that [the states] would bear if the[re] were no health reform."  See Kaiser Commission on Medicaid &amp; the Uninsured, Medicaid Coverage &amp; Spending in Health Reform 16 (May 2010).  Thus there can be no objection to the ACA's expansion of Medicaid as an "unfunded mandate."  Quite the contrary, the program is impressively well funded.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNopinion1.16" href="#FRopinion1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In 1972, for example, Congress ended the federal cash-assistance program for the aged, blind, and disabled.  That program previously had been operated jointly by the Federal and State Governments, as is the case with Medicaid today.  Congress replaced the cooperative federal program with the nationalized Supplemental Security Income (SSI) program.  See <em>Schweiker</em> v. <em>Gray Panthers</em>, 453 U.&nbsp;S. 34, 38 (1981).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNopinion1.17" href="#FRopinion1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> and the joint dissenters perceive in cooperative federalism a "threa[t]" to "political accountability."  <em>Ante</em>, at<em> </em>48; see <em>post,</em> at 34-35.  By that, they mean voter confusion: Citizens upset by unpopular government action, they posit, may ascribe to state officials blame more appropriately laid at Congress' door.  But no such confusion is apparent in this case: Medicaid's status as a federally funded, state-administered program is hardly hidden from view.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNopinion1.18" href="#FRopinion1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although the plaintiffs, in the proceedings below, did not contest the ACA's satisfaction of these criteria, see 648 F.&nbsp;3d 1235, 1263 (CA11 2011), <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> appears to rely heavily on the second criterion.  Compare <em>ante</em>, at 52, 54, with <em>infra</em>, at 52-54.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNopinion1.19" href="#FRopinion1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Compare Subchapter XIX, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1396-1396v(b) (2006 ed. and Supp. IV) with 1396a(a) (10)(A)(i)(VIII) (2006 ed. and Supp. IV); 1396a(a) (10)(A)(ii)(XX), 1396a(a)(75), 1396a(k), 1396a(gg) to (hh), 1396d(y), 1396r-1(e), 1396u-7(b)(5) to (6).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNopinion1.20" href="#FRopinion1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Deficit Reduction Act of 2005 authorized States to provide "benchmark coverage" or "benchmark equivalent coverage" to certain Medicaid populations.  See 6044, 120 Stat. 88, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1396u-7 (2006 ed. and Supp. IV).  States may offer the same level of coverage to persons newly eligible under the ACA.  See 1396a(k).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNopinion1.21" href="#FRopinion1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> observes that "Spending Clause legislation [i]s much in the nature of a <em>contract</em>."  <em>Ante</em>, at 46 (internal quotation marks omitted).  See also <em>post,</em> at 33 (joint opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kennedy</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, JJ.) (same).  But the Court previously has recognized that "[u]nlike normal contractual undertakings, federal grant programs originate in and remain governed by statutory provisions expressing the judgment of Congress concerning desirable public policy."  <em>Bennett</em> v. <em>Kentucky Dept. of Ed.</em>, 470 U.&nbsp;S. 656, 669 (1985).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Title I of the Elementary and Secondary Education Act of 1965 provided federal grants to finance supplemental educational programs in school districts with high concentrations of children from low-income families.  See <em>Bennett</em> v. <em>New Jersey</em>, 470 U.&nbsp;S. 632, 634-635 (1985) (citing Pub. L. No. 89-10, 79 Stat. 27).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Note, in this regard, the extension of Social Security, which began in 1935 as an old-age pension program, then expanded to include survivor benefits in 1939 and disability benefits in 1956.  See Social Security Act, ch. 531, 49 Stat. 622-625; Social Security Act Amendments of 1939, 53 Stat. 1364-1365; Social Security Amendments of 1956, ch. 836,  103, 70 Stat. 815-816.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The joint dissenters, for their part, would make this the entire inquiry.  "[I]f States<em> </em>really have no choice other than to accept the package," they assert, "the offer is coercive."  <em>Post,</em> at 35.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> recognizes Congress' authority to construct a single federal program and "condition the receipt of funds on the States' complying with restrictions on the use of those funds."  <em>Ante</em>, at 50.  For the joint dissenters, however, all that matters, it appears, is whether States can resist the temptation of a given federal grant.  <em>Post,</em> at 35.  On this logic, any federal spending program, sufficiently large and well-funded, would be unconstitutional.  The joint dissenters point to smaller programs States might have the will to refuse.  See <em>post,</em> at<em> </em>40-41 (elementary and secondary education).  But how is a court to judge whether "only<em> </em>6.6% of all state expenditures," <em>post, </em>at 41, is an amount States could or would do without?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Speculations of this genre are characteristic of the joint dissent.  See, <em>e.g., post</em>, at 35 ("it <em>may</em> be state officials who will bear the brunt of public disapproval" for joint federal-state endeavors); <em>ibid.</em>, ("federal officials .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. <em>may </em>remain insulated from the electoral ramifications of their decision"); <em>post, </em>at 37 ("a heavy federal tax .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. levied to support a federal program that offers large grants to the States .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. <em>may</em>, as a practical matter, [leave States] unable to refuse to participate"); <em>ibid. </em>(withdrawal from a federal program "would <em>likely</em> force the State to impose a huge tax increase"); <em>post,</em> at 46 (state share of ACA expansion costs "<em>may </em>increase in the future") (all emphasis added; some internal quotation marks omitted).  The joint dissenters are long on conjecture and short on real-world examples.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The joint dissenters also rely heavily on Congress' perceived intent to coerce the States.  <em>Post, </em>at 42-46; see, <em>e.g., post,</em> at 42 ("In crafting the ACA, Congress clearly expressed its informed view that no State could possibly refuse the offer that the ACA extends.").  We should not lightly ascribe to Congress an intent to violate the Constitution (at least as my colleagues read it).  This is particularly true when the ACA could just as well be comprehended as demonstrating Congress' mere expectation, in light of the uniformity of past participation and the generosity of the federal contribution, that States would not withdraw.  Cf. <em>South Dakota</em> v. <em>Dole</em>, 483 U.&nbsp;S. 203, 211 (1987) ("We cannot conclude .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. that a conditional grant of federal money .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. is unconstitutional simply by reason of its success in achieving the congressional objective.").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Federal taxation of a State's citizens, according to the joint dissenters, may diminish a State's ability to raise new revenue.  This, in turn, could limit a State's capacity to replace a federal program with an "equivalent" state-funded analog.  <em>Post,</em> at 40.  But it cannot be true that "the amount of the federal taxes extracted from the taxpayers of a State to pay for the program in question is relevant in determining whether there is impermissible coercion."  <em>Post,</em> at 37.  When the United States Government taxes United States citizens, it taxes them "in their individual capacities" as "the people of America"--not as residents of a particular State.  See <em>U.&nbsp;S. Term Limits, Inc.</em> v. <em>Thornton</em>, 514 U.&nbsp;S. 779, 839 (1995) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kennedy</small></span>, J., concurring).  That is because the "Framers split the atom of sovereignty[,] .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. establishing two orders of government"--"one state and one federal"--"each with its own direct relationship" to the people.  <em>Id.,</em> at 838.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A State therefore has no claim on the money its residents pay in federal taxes, and federal "spending programs need not help people in all states in the same measure."  See Brief for David Satcher et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 19.  In 2004, for example, New Jersey received 55 cents in federal spending for every dollar its residents paid to the Federal Government in taxes, while Mississippi received $1.77 per tax dollar paid.  C. Dubay, Tax Foundation, Federal Tax Burdens and Expenditures by State: Which States Gain Most from Federal Fiscal Operations?  2 (Mar. 2006).  Thus no constitutional problem was created when Arizona declined for 16 years to participate in Medicaid, even though its residents' tax dollars financed Medicaid programs in every other State.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNopinion1.27" href="#FRopinion1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> observes, the Secretary is authorized to withhold all of a State's Medicaid funding.  See <em>ante</em>, at 51.  But total withdrawal is what the Secretary <em>may</em>, not must, do.  She has discretion to withhold only a portion of the Medicaid funds otherwise due a noncompliant State.  See 1396c; cf. 45 CFR 80.10(f) (2011) (Secretary may enforce Title VI's nondiscrimination requirement through "refusal to grant or continue Federal financial assistance, <em>in whole or in part</em>." (emphasis added)).  The Secretary, it is worth noting, may herself experience political pressures, which would make her all the more reluctant to cut off funds Congress has appropriated for a State's needy citizens.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The most authoritative legal dictionaries of the founding era lack any definition for "regulate" or "regulation," suggesting that the term bears its ordinary meaning (rather than some specialized legal meaning) in the constitutional text.  See R. Burn, A New Law Dictionary 281 (1792); G. Jacob, A New Law Dictionary (10th ed. 1782); 2 T. Cunningham, A New and Complete Law Dictionary (2d ed. 1771).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span> is therefore right to note that Congress is "not mandating the purchase of a discrete, unwanted product."  <em>Ante,</em> at 22 (opinion concurring in part, concurring in judgment in part, and dis  senting in part).  Instead, it is mandating the purchase of an unwanted suite of products--<em>e.g., </em>physician office visits, emergency room visits, hospital room and board, physical therapy, durable medical equipment, mental health care, and substance abuse detoxification.  See Selected Medical Benefits: A Report from the Dept. of Labor to the Dept. of Health &amp; Human Services (April 15, 2011) (reporting that over two-thirds of private industry health plans cover these goods and services), online at http://www.bls.gov/ncs/ebs/sp/selmedbensreport.pdf (all Internet materials as visited June 26, 2012, and available in Clerk of Court's case file).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In its effort to show the contrary, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg's</small></span> dissent comes  up with nothing more than two condemnation cases, which it says demonstrate "Congress' authority under the commerce power to compel an 'inactive' landholder to submit to an unwanted sale."  <em>Ante, </em>at 24.  Wrong on both scores.  As its name suggests, the condemnation power does not "compel" anyone to do anything.  It acts <em>in&nbsp;rem</em>, against the property that is condemned, and is effective with or without a transfer of title from the former owner.  More important, the power to condemn for public use is a separate sovereign power, explicitly acknowledged in the Fifth Amendment, which provides that "private property [shall not] be taken for public use, without just compensation."</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Thus, the power to condemn tends to refute rather than support the power to compel purchase of unwanted goods at a prescribed price: The latter is rather like the power to condemn cash for public use.  If it existed, why would it not (like the condemnation power) be accompanied by a requirement of fair compensation for the portion of the exacted price that exceeds the goods' fair market value (here, the difference between what the free market would charge for a health-insurance policy on a young, healthy person with no pre-existing conditions, and the government-exacted community-rated premium)?</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;No one seriously contends that any of Congress' other enumerated powers gives it the authority to enact 5000A <em>as a regulation</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Of course it can be both for statutory purposes, since Congress can define "tax" and "penalty" in its enactments any way it wishes.  That is why <em>United States</em> v. <em>Sotelo</em>, 436 U.&nbsp;S. 268 (1978), does not disprove our statement.  That case held that a "penalty" for willful failure to pay one's taxes was included among the "taxes" made non-dischargeable under the Bankruptcy Code.  436 U.&nbsp;S., at 273-275.  Whether the "penalty" was a "tax" within the meaning of the Bankruptcy Code had absolutely no bearing on whether it escaped the constitutional limitations on penalties.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The <em>amicus</em> appointed to defend the proposition that the Anti-Injunction Act deprives us of jurisdiction stresses that the penalty for failing to comply with the mandate "shall be assessed and collected in the same manner as an assessable penalty under subchapter B of chapter 68," 26 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 5000A(g)(1) (2006 ed., Supp. IV), and that such penalties "shall be assessed and collected in the same manner as taxes," 6671(a) (2006 ed.).  But that point seems to us to confirm the <em>inapplicability</em> of the Anti-Injunction Act.  That the penalty is to be "assessed and collected <em>in the same manner as</em> taxes" refutes the proposition that it <em>is </em>a<em> </em>tax for all statutory purposes, including with respect to the Anti-Injunction Act.  Moreover, elsewhere in the Internal Revenue Code, Congress has provided <em>both</em> that a particular payment shall be "assessed and collected" in the same manner as a tax <em>and</em> that no suit shall be maintained to restrain the assessment or collection of the payment.  See, <em>e.g., </em>7421(b)(1), 6901(a); 6305(a), (b).  The  latter directive would be superfluous if the former invoked the Anti-Injunction Act.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Amicus </em>also suggests that the penalty should be treated as a tax because it is an assessable penalty, and the Code's assessment provision authorizes the Secretary of the Treasury to assess "all taxes (including interest, additional amounts, additions to the tax, and assessable penalties) imposed by this title."  6201(a) (2006 ed., Supp. IV).  But the fact that such items are included as "taxes" for purposes of assessment does not establish that they are included as "taxes" for purposes of other sections of the Code, such as the Anti-Injunction Act, that do not contain similar "including" language.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"State expenditures" is used here to mean annual expenditures from the States' own funding sources, and it excludes federal grants unless otherwise noted.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This number is expressed in billions of Fiscal Year 2005 dollars.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Office of Management and Budget, Historical Tables, Budget of the U.&nbsp;S. Government, Fiscal Year 2013, Table 12.1--Summary Comparison of Total Outlays for Grants to State and Local Governments: 1940-2017 (hereinafter Table 12.1), http://www.whitehouse.gov/omb/ budget/Historicals; <em>id</em>., Table 15.2--Total Government Expenditures: 1948-2011 (hereinafter Table 15.2).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This number is expressed in billions of Fiscal Year 2005 dollars.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Table 12.1; Dept. of Commerce, Bureau of Census, Statistical Abstract of the United States: 2001, p. 262 (Table 419, Federal Grants-in-Aid Summary: 1970 to 2001).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Statistical Abstract of the United States: 2012, p. 268 (Table 431, Federal Grants-in-Aid to State and Local Governments: 1990 to 2011).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg</small></span> argues that "[a] State .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. has no claim on the money its residents pay in federal taxes."  <em>Ante,</em> at 59, n. 26.  This is true as a formal matter.  "When the United States Government taxes United States citizens, it taxes them 'in their individual capacities' as 'the people of America'--not as residents of a particular State."  <em>Ante,</em> at 58, n. 26 (quoting <em>U. S. Term Limits, Inc.</em> v. <em>Thornton</em>, 514 U. S. 779, 839 (1995) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kennedy, J</small></span>., concurring)).  But unless <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg</small></span> thinks that there is no limit to the amount of money that can be squeezed out of taxpayers, heavy federal taxation diminishes the practical ability of States to collect their own taxes.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Federal Government has a higher number for federal spending on Medicaid.  According to the Office of Management and Budget, federal grants to the States for Medicaid amounted to nearly $273 billion in Fiscal Year 2010.  See Office of Management and Budget, Historical Tables, Budget of the U. S. Government, Fiscal Year 2013, Table 12.3--Total Outlays for Grants to State and Local Governments by Function, Agency, and Program:  1940-2013, http://www.whitehouse.gov/omb/budget/Historicals.  In that Fiscal Year, total federal outlays for grants to state and local governments amounted to over $608 billion, see Table 12.1, and state and local government expenditures from their own sources amounted to $1.6 trillion, see Table 15.2.  Using these numbers, 44.8% of all federal outlays to both state and local governments was allocated to Medicaid, amounting to 16.8% of all state and local expenditures from their own sources.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Federal Government reports a higher percentage.  According to Medicaid.gov, in Fiscal Year 2010, the Federal Government made Medicaid payments in the amount of nearly $260 billion, representing 67.79% of total Medicaid payments of $383 billion.  See www.medicaid.gov/Medicaid-CHIP-Program-Information/By-State/By-State.html.</p>

_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

_satellite.ads.sendConversionFindLaw = function() {
  var subId = _satellite.getVar('current_ad_eng').sub;
  var cookieName = 'fl_ad_'+subId;
  var cookieValue = 'true';
  var c = _satellite.cookie.get(cookieName);
  // we use a cookie to ensure multiple beacons are not sent for the same ad
  if( typeof c == 'undefined' ) {
    _satellite.setFlCookie(cookieName, cookieValue, 1);

    var data = {};
    var clickUrlElement = document.getElementById(_satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub'));
    if (clickUrlElement !== null) {
      data.clickUrl = clickUrlElement.innerText;
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') ) {
      data.adDecisionContext = _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id') ) {
      data.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id');
    }

      data.subId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id') ) {
      data.flUserId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_tag_track') == 'false' ) {
      data.internalFlag = 'true'; // mimick as an internal request
    }

    let url = new URL(window.location.href);
    let domain = 'attorneys.findlaw'+(_satellite.getVar('cms_env') == 'prod' ? '' : '-stage')+'.com';
    let attorneysUrl = new URL('https://'+domain+'/flstats/v1/stats');
    let searchParams = new URLSearchParams(window.location.search);

    if (searchParams.has('ads-server-env')) {
      attorneysUrl.searchParams.set('ads-server-env', url.searchParams.get('ads-server-env'));
    }
    navigator.sendBeacon(attorneysUrl.href, JSON.stringify(data));
  }
}

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

</script><script>_satellite["_runScript5"](function(event, target, Promise) {
window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });
    }
    
    //data-search-location
    const linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);
    linksWithSearchLocation.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = e.currentTarget.dataset.searchLocation || e.currentTarget.dataset.closest("[data-search-location]").dataset.searchLocation;
        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });

    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
